26. How to implement pooling and resource management for heavy gateway clients in Bridge?

Answer: Gateway implementor manages client pooling (HTTP client pools); keep resource handling internal to implementor and expose thread-safe methods for charge().

27. How to version the Bridge interfaces in a stable API?

Answer: Version PaymentGateway contract via package or interface version suffix (e.g., PaymentGatewayV1). Provide adapters for older implementations; migrate consumers gradually.

28. How to support synchronous and asynchronous charging in Bridge?

Answer: Define PaymentGateway to return PaymentResult that can indicate PENDING and include correlation id; abstraction persists record and saga listens for callback. Alternatively provide async API variant returning CompletableFuture<PaymentResult>.

29. How to do canary deployments of a new gateway where Bridge is used?

Answer: Register new gateway and route a small percentage of traffic via routing layer/feature flag. Use metrics to compare success/latency and rollback if problems.

30. How to handle idempotency across Bridge boundary?

Answer: Include idempotency key in gateway call (provided by abstraction). Gateways should honor key; if not, maintain idempotency at service level with persisted request ledger.

31. How to model multi-currency payments in Bridge?

Answer: Abstraction handles currency conversion logic or delegates to a CurrencyService. Gateways accept amount+currency — ensure gateway implementors support required currencies.

32. How to decide which side (abstraction or implementor) handles logging or auditing?

Answer: Abstraction logs business-level events (attempt, success, failure); implementor logs protocol-level traces (raw request/response). Use consistent correlation id across both.

33. How to migrate from monolithic gateway code to Bridge?

Answer: Introduce PaymentGateway interface and wrap existing monolithic code in a LegacyGatewayAdapter implementing the interface. Gradually extract and refactor.

34. How to ensure concurrency safety when multiple threads call Payment concurrently?

Answer: Ensure Payment subclasses are stateless or thread-safe (no mutable shared fields). Gateways use thread-safe HTTP clients and avoid per-call shared mutable state.

35. How to add new payment behaviors like scheduled recurring payments with Bridge?

Answer: Implement RecurringPayment as abstraction using a PaymentGateway for charging. Recurrence scheduler invokes abstraction; gateway implementation handles actual charge.

36. How to model composite operations (authorize + capture) in Bridge?

Answer: Extend PaymentGateway to support authorize() and capture() methods. Abstraction CardPayment may call authorize() then capture() based on flow (delayed capture).

37. How to unit-test a Bridge composition (Payment + Gateway) end-to-end in isolation?

Answer: Mock PaymentGateway and verify Payment behavior (calls, retries). For gateway tests, use sandbox or mocked HTTP server. For combined tests use real implementations in test environment.

38. How to implement observability hooks without breaking Bridge abstraction?

Answer: Use decorators for both sides (gateway decorator, payment decorator) that add metrics/tracing without changing interfaces. This keeps concerns separated.

39. How to design the PaymentGateway interface to be stable yet expressive?

Answer: Keep it minimal: methods like charge(ChargeRequest), refund(RefundRequest), status(transactionId) returning PaymentResult. Avoid vendor-specific fields on the main interface.

40. How to handle edge-case vendor requirements (e.g., 3DS redirect flow) with Bridge?

Answer: Expose a flexible PaymentResult object that can carry extra metadata (redirect URL, form data). Specialized abstractions can use this metadata to continue flow.

41. How to prevent the Bridge from becoming a leaky abstraction when gateways have incompatible semantics?

Answer: Define the abstraction contract narrowly to the intersection of capabilities; expose advanced features via optional capability interfaces or extension hooks.

42. (Tricky) How to implement a fallback payment gateway strategy in Bridge when primary fails mid-flight?

Answer: Abstraction (or higher-level orchestrator) should detect payment failure and attempt another gateway with same idempotency key and compensation logic (refund if partial). Ensure transaction semantics and reconciliation (avoid double-charge).

43. (Tricky) How to maintain data privacy while logging gateway interactions?

Answer: Redact sensitive fields (card number, tokens) at gateway decorator level. Log only masked or tokenized values and correlation ids. Use structured logs with sampling for large volumes.

44. (Tricky) How to test failure modes (timeouts, partial success) across Bridge layers?

Answer: Use mocks that simulate timeouts, slow responses, partial success, and verify Payment reactions (retries, compensation triggers). Use integration tests with a test double that simulates vendor behavior.

45. (Tricky) If multiple abstractions call same gateway concurrently and gateway has rate-limits, how to coordinate?

Answer: Introduce a gateway-side rate limiter or a shared decorator that throttles requests across all callers. Use token bucket per gateway and expose retry-after / backoff semantics to callers.

46. (Tricky) How to evolve PaymentGateway when adding new async flows without breaking existing implementations?

Answer: Add new methods as default no-op (if using Java default methods) or create a PaymentGatewayV2 interface and provide adaptors for older implementations. Use adapter classes to translate.

47. (Tricky) Bridge & multi-tenancy: different tenants require different gateway contracts — how handle?

Answer: Use tenant-aware gateway factories producing gateway instances configured per tenant. Abstraction remains same; factory chooses gateway and configuration (credentials, endpoints) per tenant.

48. (Tricky) How to implement graceful degradation when no gateway supports a requested feature (e.g., currency)?

Answer: Fall back to alternative flows: notify user, offer supported currency, queue order for manual processing, or route to a conversion service. Keep user experience clear and auditable.

49. (Tricky) How to combine Bridge with Adapter and Decorator patterns in payment pipeline?

Answer: Adapter can adapt vendor SDK to PaymentGateway interface, Decorator adds logging/retry/idempotency, and Bridge composes Payment abstraction with decorated gateway. This combination preserves separation of concerns.

50. Summarize best practices and pitfalls for Bridge in OrderService

Answer (checklist):

Keep PaymentGateway small and stable.

Put business logic in Payment abstractions, protocol detail in gateways.

Use DI/factories to wire combinations.

Use decorators for retries, logging, circuit-breakers (don’t bloat gateway).

Persist idempotency keys and correlate calls across retries/failovers.

Instrument both sides (metrics, traces) with shared correlation id.

Prefer composition over inheritance; keep classes focused (SRP).

Use capability patterns for optional vendor features.

Use contract tests for gateway implementations.

Plan for region/tenant configuration and for non-blocking async flows via saga/orchestrator.



50 In-Depth Interview Q&A — Composite (OrderService examples)

Context: Composite treats individual objects and groups uniformly. In OrderService, a Bundle (gift box, combo pack) contains other OrderComponents, but client code calls price() the same way.

interface OrderComponent { double price(); }
class OrderItem implements OrderComponent {
  String name; int qty; double unitPrice;
  public double price(){ return qty * unitPrice; }
}
class Bundle implements OrderComponent {
  List<OrderComponent> children = new ArrayList<>();
  public double price(){ return children.stream().mapToDouble(OrderComponent::price).sum(); }
}

1. What problem does the Composite pattern solve in OrderService?

Answer: It allows treating individual OrderItem and Bundle uniformly. Clients (like billing) call price() without knowing if the object is a single item or nested bundle.

2. How does Composite demonstrate polymorphism?

Answer: Both OrderItem and Bundle implement OrderComponent. Calls to price() are dispatched polymorphically, even though implementations differ.

3. Which SOLID principles does Composite support?

Answer: OCP (add new components like GiftBundle without changing client), LSP (any component can replace another), SRP (each class has one responsibility: leaf or composite behavior).

4. What’s the difference between Composite and Decorator?

Answer: Composite represents part-whole hierarchies (a bundle of items). Decorator adds extra behavior to a single object without changing its interface. Composite = tree, Decorator = wrapper.

5. Show how to compute total price with bundles inside bundles.
Bundle giftBox = new Bundle();
giftBox.add(new OrderItem("Book",1,200));
Bundle stationery = new Bundle();
stationery.add(new OrderItem("Pen",5,10));
giftBox.add(stationery);
System.out.println(giftBox.price()); // 250

6. How to enforce immutability in a Composite?

Answer: Make children unmodifiable after construction, and provide builder/factory for controlled creation. This prevents external mutation of composite structure.

7. How to handle discounts at bundle level?

Answer: Add field discountPercent in Bundle. price() applies discount after summing child prices. Keep logic localized in composite class.

8. How to add validation for cycles in a composite tree?

Answer: During add(child), check if child already exists in ancestor chain. Throw exception on cycle detection to prevent infinite recursion.

9. How to test a composite hierarchy?

Answer: Build sample structures with nested bundles, compute expected price manually, assert equality. Test deep nesting and empty bundle cases.

10. How to serialize composite structures for APIs?

Answer: Use JSON representation with recursive structure:

{ "type":"bundle", "children":[ { "type":"item","name":"Book","qty":1,"unitPrice":200 } ] }


Ensure recursion is handled and prevent cycles.

11. Composite vs Aggregate Root in DDD?

Answer: Composite is a design pattern for tree-like structures. Aggregate root is a DDD concept for transactional boundaries. A bundle could be part of an aggregate, but not all composites are aggregates.

12. How to handle large composites efficiently?

Answer: Use caching/memoization of total price, update cache incrementally on child changes. For very large trees, compute asynchronously or stream.

13. How to make composite operations thread-safe?

Answer: Synchronize access to children list, use concurrent collections, or treat composites as immutable after construction.

14. How to extend Composite with operations like countItems()?

Answer: Add method to interface or use Visitor pattern to traverse and compute metrics. Visitor is better if many new operations are needed.

15. How to keep Composite testable when mapping from external product catalogs?

Answer: Separate mapping logic: map vendor bundles → our Bundle structure in adapter. Unit-test mapping layer independently.

16. How to support both flat and hierarchical orders in the same service?

Answer: Expose API expecting OrderComponent. Internally, use a Bundle with one child for flat orders, enabling uniform processing.

17. How to prevent Composite from becoming a God class?

Answer: Don’t add all operations (ship, notify) into OrderComponent. Keep it small (e.g., only price, weight). Use Visitors or external services for additional behavior.

18. How to handle different currencies in composite pricing?

Answer: Components return price in their currency. Composite normalizes via injected CurrencyService before summing.

19. How to implement equality/hashCode for composite structures?

Answer: Base on structure: compare children recursively. Be careful with cycles. For business equality, compare normalized price/ids.

20. What are risks of deep recursion in composites?

Answer: Stack overflow if structure is too deep. Mitigate by iterative traversal or tail recursion optimization if language supports it.

21. How to add new component types without changing client code?

Answer: Add class implementing OrderComponent (e.g., ServiceFee) and override price(). Clients treat it uniformly.

22. How to integrate Composite with Builder?

Answer: Use BundleBuilder with methods .addItem() and .addBundle(). Simplifies construction of large composites.

23. Composite vs Chain of Responsibility?

Answer: Composite = structural tree. CoR = sequential processing chain. Composite combines results; CoR passes along a request until handled.

24. How to implement visitor traversal of composite tree?
interface Visitor { void visit(OrderItem i); void visit(Bundle b); }
class PriceVisitor implements Visitor { ... }


Call accept(visitor) on each component, recursing bundles.

25. How to enforce business rules (bundle must contain ≥2 items)?

Answer: Validate in Bundle.add() method. Throw InvalidBundleException if constraint not met.

26. How to integrate discounts at item vs bundle level without duplication?

Answer: Apply item-level discount in OrderItem.price(), bundle-level discount in Bundle.price(). Compose discounts carefully to avoid double-counting.

27. How to handle performance monitoring in composite traversal?

Answer: Instrument price() calls. Add counters for depth and number of items processed. Optimize if hot path.

28. How to store composite in relational DB?

Answer: Use parent-child table with foreign keys. Recursively query bundles and children. For deep trees, use adjacency list or nested sets.

29. How to handle security concerns (exposing child items in API)?

Answer: Add filtering before serialization. Only expose child items if user authorized to see details.

30. How to test bundle-level invariants automatically?

Answer: Write property-based tests generating random bundles and checking invariants (e.g., non-negative total, no cycles).

31. How to support undo/redo for bundle composition?

Answer: Use Memento to snapshot composite structure before modification. Restore snapshot to undo changes.

32. Composite vs Aggregator microservice pattern?

Answer: Composite is an OO design pattern. Aggregator in microservices composes responses from multiple services. Similar concept but different scale.

33. How to avoid excessive object creation in large composites?

Answer: Reuse immutable leaf components (Flyweight). Example: common OrderItem like "GiftWrap" reused across bundles.

34. How to ensure pricing consistency across nested bundles?

Answer: Apply rounding consistently at leaf level. Ensure composite sums use BigDecimal to avoid floating-point drift.

35. How to expose composite APIs externally (REST/GraphQL)?

Answer: GraphQL naturally supports recursive queries for bundle structure. For REST, use nested JSON arrays. Provide pagination for very large composites.

36. How to implement caching in composites?

Answer: Cache price() at bundle level. Invalidate cache on child modification. For immutables, compute once and store.

37. How to log traversal of composites for debugging?

Answer: Use visitor pattern to log each node visited with indentation. Helps visualize bundle structure in logs.

38. How to parallelize computation over large composites?

Answer: Use parallel streams/ForkJoin to compute price() concurrently for independent child bundles. Ensure thread-safety.

39. How to integrate with Observer (event per child addition)?

Answer: Bundle can publish event ChildAdded when add() is called. Observers can enforce business rules or update UI.

40. How to support lazy-loading children in a composite?

Answer: Implement Bundle with proxy children list that loads from DB on first access. Ensure transparent traversal for clients.

41. Composite vs Decorator in discounts?

Answer: Decorator adds discount behavior around item; Composite groups items and applies group discount. They can be combined.

42. How to serialize composite safely without infinite recursion?

Answer: Use cycle-detection (visited set). For JSON, use frameworks with cycle handling annotations (e.g., Jackson @JsonIdentityInfo).

43. How to implement UI rendering of composite (nested bundles)?

Answer: Traverse recursively, rendering tree structure with indentation. In web UI, render nested cards/lists.

44. How to model composite in GraphQL schema?

Answer:

type OrderComponent { id: ID!, type: String!, price: Float!, children: [OrderComponent] }


Supports recursion naturally.

45. How to integrate composite pricing with promotions?

Answer: Promotions engine can traverse composite tree applying rules (bundle discount, free item). Use Visitor or Strategy pattern for promotions.

46. How to implement fail-fast for invalid children in composite?

Answer: Validate child type in add(). Throw exception immediately rather than allow invalid state to propagate.

47. How to store and retrieve composite snapshots in event sourcing?

Answer: Store BundleCreated, ItemAdded events. Reconstruct composite by replaying events. Snapshots optimize rebuild.

48. How to scale composites across microservices?

Answer: Represent bundles as references. Leaf items may come from product service, bundle structure from order service. Compose at API layer.

49. What tricky interview pitfall exists for Composite?

Answer: Interviewers may ask: “How to add a new operation (e.g., weight) without modifying all classes?” Answer: Use Visitor pattern instead of bloating OrderComponent.


50 In-Depth Interview Q&A — Decorator (OrderService examples)

Context / canonical example: We wrap a PaymentProcessor with decorators to add behavior without changing the concrete processor:

interface PaymentProcessor { String pay(Order order) throws Exception; }

class StripePaymentProcessor implements PaymentProcessor { /* core */ }

class LoggingPaymentDecorator implements PaymentProcessor {
  private final PaymentProcessor delegate;
  LoggingPaymentDecorator(PaymentProcessor delegate){ this.delegate = delegate; }
  public String pay(Order o) throws Exception {
    log("before", o);
    String r = delegate.pay(o);
    log("after", o, r);
    return r;
  }
}

1 — (S) What is the Decorator pattern and why use it in OrderService?

Answer: Decorator attaches additional responsibilities to objects dynamically. In OrderService, decorators add cross-cutting concerns (logging, fraud checks, retries, metrics) to a PaymentProcessor without modifying the processor.

2 — (S) How does a LoggingPaymentDecorator work?

Answer: It wraps a PaymentProcessor, logs before and after calling delegate.pay(order), and returns the delegate’s result. The decorator implements the same interface, preserving substitution.

3 — (S) Why prefer Decorator over inheritance here?

Answer: Decorator composes behavior at runtime and avoids subclass explosion (don’t create StripeWithLoggingWithFraud classes). It keeps behavior modular and testable.

4 — (I) How to chain multiple decorators (order matters)?

Answer: Compose them explicitly: new FraudCheckDecorator(new LoggingDecorator(new StripeProcessor())). Order matters: placing fraud before logging may prevent logging of blocked calls or vice versa depending on desired behavior.

5 — (I) How to wire decorators in Spring?

Answer: Provide a @Configuration bean that composes them:

@Bean PaymentProcessor paymentProcessor(StripePaymentProcessor stripe){
  return new FraudCheckPaymentDecorator(new LoggingPaymentDecorator(stripe));
}


Inject this decorated bean where needed.

6 — (I) How to unit test a decorator in isolation?

Answer: Wrap a mock PaymentProcessor with the decorator and verify interactions (e.g., verify(mock).pay(order)), behavior on exceptions, and that logging or fraud checks behave as expected.

7 — (I) Should decorators be stateful or stateless?

Answer: Prefer stateless decorators. If state is needed (e.g., per-customer rate counters), make the state thread-safe (AtomicLong, ConcurrentHashMap) or move state to a dedicated service.

8 — (I) How to implement a RetryPaymentDecorator safely regarding idempotency?

Answer: Only retry on transient errors and include an idempotency key in payment requests so retries do not double-charge. Limit retry count and use exponential backoff.

9 — (I) How to add metrics via a decorator?

Answer: Create MetricsPaymentDecorator that records latency and success/failure counters using Micrometer around delegate.pay().

10 — (I) How to handle exceptions inside decorators (should they swallow or propagate)?

Answer: Generally propagate after adding context or logging. If a decorator wants to convert exceptions (e.g., map vendor exceptions to domain ones), do so explicitly. Swallowing is dangerous unless deliberate fallback behavior is implemented.

11 — (I) When to use AOP instead of Decorator?

Answer: Use AOP for broad cross-cutting concerns (tracing, security) applied across many classes. Use Decorators when you need instance-level or runtime composition of behavior and explicit ordering.

12 — (I) How to test decorator order interactions?

Answer: Use Mockito InOrder to assert call ordering on delegate and side effects triggered by decorators.

13 — (I) How to ensure thread-safety for decorators that maintain counters?

Answer: Use thread-safe primitives (AtomicLong, ConcurrentHashMap) and minimize contention; consider sharded counters or Micrometer which is thread-safe.

14 — (I) How to add configuration to enable/disable a decorator (e.g., fraud check in dev)?

Answer: Use Spring @ConditionalOnProperty to register decorator bean only when enabled; or compose factory that conditionally wraps based on config.

15 — (M) (Senior) How to implement decorator-based rate limiting per customer?

Answer: Decorator maintains per-customer rate state using a token-bucket implementation keyed by customer id. Use Redis or a distributed rate-limiter for multi-node consistency if necessary.

16 — (M) How to implement a decorator that enforces circuit-breaker semantics?

Answer: Use Resilience4j; create a CircuitBreakerDecorator that consults the CircuitBreaker before calling delegate.pay(), short-circuits on open state and triggers fallback or exception.

17 — (M) When chaining decorators, how to ensure errors provide meaningful telemetry?

Answer: Decorators should attach context (order id, correlation id) to exceptions or logs. Use structured logs and include decorator names to trace the pipeline.

18 — (M) How to implement decorator composition dynamically (e.g., change at runtime)?

Answer: Use a decorator factory or a dynamic proxy that builds the chain based on runtime config, feature flags, or user attributes. Reconfigure via hot-reloadable config and swap bean instance atomically.

19 — (M) How to avoid performance overhead when stacking many decorators?

Answer: Keep decorators lightweight and avoid blocking operations inside them. Combine multiple trivial decorators (metrics+logging) into a single optimized decorator if profiling shows overhead.

20 — (M) How to handle cross-cutting state propagation between decorators (e.g., fraud decision passed to logging)?

Answer: Use a transient context object passed through the call (e.g., PaymentContext) or use MDC for log correlation. Decorators can mutate context to share info without using shared mutable fields.

21 — (M) How to implement decorator for async payment flows?

Answer: For async CompletableFuture returns, decorator should compose futures:

public CompletableFuture<String> pay(Order o) {
  logBefore();
  return delegate.pay(o).whenComplete((r, ex) -> logAfter(r, ex));
}


Ensure exception handling and timeouts are correct.

22 — (M) Decorator vs Proxy — relationship and differences?

Answer: Both wrap objects. Decorator adds responsibilities dynamically and can be stacked; Proxy controls access and can add remote/virtualization concerns. Practically they’re similar; intent differs.

23 — (M) How to handle decorator testing in integration tests?

Answer: Spin up decorated bean (full chain) in Spring test context and assert end-to-end behavior, including metrics, logs, and external calls (mocked).

24 — (M) How to make decorators composable with dependency injection when multiple implementations exist?

Answer: Inject the base implementation and then register decorators that wrap it in a known order inside @Configuration. Alternatively, use Ordered beans and an assembling factory that applies decorators by order.

25 — (M) How to implement a security decorator that validates permissions per order?

Answer: Decorator checks caller identity and permissions before delegating. If unauthorized, throw AccessDeniedException. Keep checks fast and rely on cached authorization policies when possible.

26 — (M) How to implement a decorator that collects and persists audit events?

Answer: The AuditDecorator records necessary details before/after delegate call and persists an audit record (async for performance). Ensure it is durable and non-blocking (use append-only logs or event queues).

27 — (M) How to instrument decorators for distributed tracing?

Answer: Start/continue a tracing span in the decorator, set attributes (orderId, amount), and end span after delegate completes. For nested decorators keep a single span for the whole chain or separate spans per decorator for detailed breakdown.

28 — (M) How to design a decorator for graceful degradation (fallback logic)?

Answer: FallbackDecorator catches specific exceptions, and either returns a safe fallback result or delegates to a fallback processor. Ensure fallbacks are idempotent and safe.

29 — (M) How to handle retry in decorator but still preserve idempotency?

Answer: Decorator should generate and pass idempotency key to delegate and perform retries only for transient exceptions. Maintain retry metadata in persistent store if long-running.

30 — (M) When to use static decorators vs dynamic proxies (JDK/ByteBuddy)?

Answer: Use static decorators when composition is simple and explicit. Use dynamic proxies when you need flexible runtime wiring across many interfaces or for AOP-like behavior without writing wrappers.

31 — (T) (Tricky) If a decorator modifies the order object before passing it down, how to prevent other callers from seeing mutated state?

Answer: Use a copy or immutable PaymentContext object. Either clone the Order for downstream or use a context object that carries transformed values to avoid mutating shared domain objects.

32 — (T) (Tricky) How to debug decorator chains in production when behavior varies by chain ordering?

Answer: Log the decorator chain composition at startup (which decorators applied and their order) and include chain metadata in traces. Correlate logs/traces by order id to reconstruct path per request.

33 — (T) (Tricky) If a decorator failure must trigger compensation (e.g., logging fails but payment succeeded), how to manage that?

Answer: Decorators should not cause irreversible side effects that block success. For critical decorators, use best-effort (async persistence) with retry; if a decorator fails, log error and schedule compensation or reconciliation where possible.

34 — (T) (Tricky) How to avoid decorator-induced memory leaks?

Answer: Avoid storing per-request state in decorator fields; use local variables or request-scoped context. If caching in decorator, use bounded caches and eviction.

35 — (T) (Tricky) How to safely decorate methods with multiple signatures (overloaded pay methods)?

Answer: Ensure the decorator implements all interface methods and applies consistent behavior for each overload. Prefer a single semantic entry (e.g., pay(PaymentContext)) to reduce overload confusion.

36 — (T) (Tricky) How to preserve exception types across decorator layers (so callers can react appropriately)?

Answer: Decorators should map exceptions to domain-level types and preserve the cause. Avoid wrapping everything in a generic exception that hides details; use throw new DomainException("msg", ex) where appropriate.

37 — (T) (Tricky) How to handle decorator ordering when some decorators need to run "inside" others (e.g., logging should include pre/post of fraud check)?

Answer: Compose accordingly: new LoggingDecorator(new FraudDecorator(delegate)) logs both before fraud and after fraud (depending on where logging placed). If you need fine-grained control, implement nested logging points or structured events from each decorator.

38 — (T) (Tricky) How to implement transactional semantics in decorators that perform DB writes?

Answer: Let the service layer handle @Transactional boundaries. If a decorator writes to DB, it should participate in the same transaction or use async logging with eventual consistency. Avoid starting independent transactions inside decorators unless isolated.

39 — (T) (Tricky) For a highly-available multi-node system, how to implement a decorator which requires unique global quotas?

Answer: Use a distributed coordination mechanism (Redis token buckets, distributed semaphores) inside the decorator. Avoid local counters. Ensure fallback behavior when coordination is unavailable.

40 — (T) (Tricky) How to compose decorators with different lifecycles (prototype vs singleton beans)?

Answer: Ensure that a singleton decorated bean wraps prototype dependencies via ObjectProvider or factories to avoid capturing stale prototype instances. Compose using a factory that builds decorated instance per-request if needed.

41 — (T) (Tricky) How to safely add/removes decorators at runtime without breaking in-flight requests?

Answer: Swap the decorated bean instance atomically (use volatile reference or Spring Bean refresh) so new requests see new chain while in-flight ones continue with old instance. Ensure idempotency of operations.

42 — (T) (Tricky) How to ensure decorated payment operations remain auditable and non-repudiable?

Answer: Audit before/after delegate actions including idempotency keys and signed evidence where needed. Persist audit records to tamper-evident store, and correlate with trace ids.

43 — (T) (Tricky) How to avoid tight coupling between decorators and concrete delegate implementations?

Answer: Decorators should depend only on the interface (PaymentProcessor). For behavior requiring extra info, use a small capability interface or a context object rather than downcasting to concrete class.

44 — (T) (Tricky) How to unit test decorators that create threads or schedule async tasks?

Answer: Use synchronous test executors or CompletableFuture hooks to control async behavior. Inject an Executor that is a test executor and verify scheduling and completion deterministically.

45 — (T) (Tricky) How to add a decorator that collects per-customer spending limits with eventual consistency?

Answer: Decorator updates local cache and async persistent store; enforce soft-limits locally and exact enforcement via periodic reconciliation job using the persisted totals.

46 — (T) (Tricky) How to avoid decorator stacking explosion when you have many orthogonal concerns?

Answer: Group related concerns into combined decorators where it makes sense (logging+metrics), or use aspect-oriented programming for truly global orthogonal concerns. Keep chain length manageable.

47 — (T) (Tricky) How to attach contextual metadata through the decorator chain without breaking signatures?

Answer: Use a lightweight PaymentContext object passed into pay() or thread-local MDC with careful management (clear at end). Prefer explicit context parameters over implicit thread-local state.

48 — (T) (Tricky) When a decorator depends on an external service that can degrade, how to protect the payment path?

Answer: Make decorator best-effort and non-blocking — use async writes, fallbacks, circuit-breakers, and degrade gracefully (e.g., skip non-critical decoration on service unavailability).

49 — (T) (Tricky) How to perform property-based testing for decorator chains?

Answer: Generate random order payloads and random decorator compositions (within valid combos); assert invariants (no double-charges, logs emitted, idempotency preserved). Use test doubles for external calls.


50 In-Depth Interview Q&A — Facade (OrderService examples)

Context: OrderFacade exposes a simple placeOrder(Order) API that hides the internal orchestration (pricing, persistence, payment, event publishing), while OrderSaga handles the heavier compensated workflows. Facade simplifies use for clients that don’t need saga semantics.

1 — (S) What is the Facade pattern and why use it for OrderService?

Answer: Facade provides a simplified interface to a complex subsystem. OrderFacade.placeOrder() hides multiple steps (compute price, persist, charge, publish) and provides a single entry point for UIs or simple clients, reducing coupling and simplifying client code.

2 — (S) Give a minimal Java OrderFacade method example.

Answer:

public class OrderFacade {
  public void placeOrder(Order order) {
    order.total = pricing.computeTotal(order.items);
    order.id = repo.save(order);
    payment.pay(order);
    publisher.publish(new OrderPlacedEvent(order));
  }
}


It orchestrates, not implements all steps.

3 — (S) How does Facade relate to SRP?

Answer: Facade has the single responsibility of simplifying interaction/ orchestration. It should delegate domain logic to specialized services; it shouldn’t contain business rules itself to avoid becoming a God class.

4 — (I) Facade vs Service Layer — how do they differ?

Answer: Facade simplifies a complex subsystem for external clients (thin wrapper). Service layer contains business logic and acts as boundary for domain operations. Sometimes a Facade is implemented on top of service layer to expose simplified APIs.

5 — (I) How to test OrderFacade? Unit vs integration.

Answer: Unit test: mock dependencies (pricing, payment, repo, publisher) and verify orchestration order and error handling. Integration test: spring test with real beans or test doubles (WireMock for payment), assert persistence, event publish, side effects.

6 — (I) Where to put transactional boundaries with Facade?

Answer: Prefer transactions in lower-level services (e.g., repository write), not in facade unless facade coordinates a single local transaction. For cross-service operations use saga; do not try distributed transactions in facade.

7 — (I) Should Facade swallow exceptions or propagate them?

Answer: Usually propagate domain-level exceptions or translate low-level exceptions into meaningful domain errors. Only swallow exceptions when implementing a specific “best-effort” behavior and document it.

8 — (I) How to design the facade API for idempotency?

Answer: Accept an idempotencyKey parameter and ensure facade persists a request record keyed by it to avoid duplicate processing. On duplicate calls, return previous result.

9 — (I) How to handle partial failures inside placeOrder()?

Answer: Either make facade do basic rollback (local DB rollback) or delegate to saga for compensation. Facade used for simple flows; for multi-step robust flows prefer saga/orchestrator.

10 — (I) How to expose async vs sync behaviors in the facade?

Answer: Offer both sync placeOrder() (blocks until payment) and async placeOrderAsync() returning orderId and status endpoint for polling (202 Accepted). Use eventing to notify completion.

11 — (I) How to implement rate-limiting on the facade to protect downstream services?

Answer: Add a rate-limiter decorator around facade or implement request quota checks early in facade. Use token buckets or external rate-limiters to throttle input and protect payment provider.

12 — (I) How to document façade operations for clients?

Answer: Provide OpenAPI (Swagger) definitions, explain semantic guarantees (sync/async, idempotency, retries), error codes, and examples. Include correlation-id usage.

13 — (M) (Senior) How to evolve facade when underlying services split into microservices?

Answer: Keep facade as an API gateway or edge service that orchestrates calls to microservices (pricing, inventory, payment). Offload long-running tasks to async workflows (saga) and convert synchronous facade calls to asynchronous requests where needed.

14 — (M) How to implement retries for transient failures inside facade?

Answer: Use a resilience strategy (Resilience4j) with retries only for idempotent steps. For non-idempotent steps (charges), use idempotency keys and coordinate with payment provider for safe retries.

15 — (M) How to keep facade small and not turn it into a god-class?

Answer: Delegate domain logic to services; only orchestrate. Break façade into smaller facades if it grows (e.g., BillingFacade, ShippingFacade). Use compositional design and keep single responsibility.

16 — (M) How to handle observability (logs, metrics, traces) from inside the facade?

Answer: Add structured logs with correlation/order id, instrument metrics (calls, latency, success/failure), and create tracing spans around orchestration steps. Do not log PII.

17 — (M) How to implement versioning for facade APIs?

Answer: Provide API versioning (/v1/orders, /v2/orders), maintain backward compatibility for a deprecation window, and route clients to new versions gradually.

18 — (M) How to secure facade endpoints? Authentication & authorization?

Answer: Enforce authentication (OAuth/JWT), authorize actions (who can place orders), validate input, rate-limit, and protect sensitive endpoints. Validate idempotency tokens and permissions.

19 — (M) How to make facade resilient to downstream outages?

Answer: Use circuit-breakers for calls to external systems. When payment provider down, return meaningful error or queue request for later processing. Offer graceful degradation where possible (store intent, notify user).

20 — (M) How to make a facade suitable for multi-tenant usage?

Answer: Include tenant context in facade requests, resolve tenant-specific config (pricing rules, payment provider), and ensure isolation of tenant credentials/config through DI/factory per tenant.

21 — (M) How to migrate facade from in-process event publishing to durable messaging?

Answer: Introduce an EventPublisher abstraction used by the facade. Implement in-process publisher for dev and broker-backed publisher (Kafka) for production. Use the outbox pattern to ensure atomic write+publish.

22 — (M) How to test error handling when a facade step fails midway?

Answer: Use integration tests mocking failing dependency (e.g., make payment throw) and assert that persistence did not leave inconsistent state or that saga compensation occurs. Validate logs and metrics.

23 — (M) How to integrate facade with UI/clients to show order progress?

Answer: Return immediate orderId and expose /orders/{id}/status endpoint. Publish events that update order status; use websockets or push notifications for real-time updates.

24 — (M) How to ensure transactional outbox semantics within a facade operation?

Answer: Write order and outbox event to DB within same local transaction in facade’s persistence step. A separate dispatcher reads outbox and publishes to broker, ensuring no event loss.

25 — (M) How to implement audit logging for facade operations?

Answer: Add an AuditService decorator capturing who invoked what, arguments (sanitized), result, and timestamps. Persist audit entries to immutable store and index for queries.

26 — (M) How to handle concurrency when multiple clients call a facade for same resource?

Answer: Use optimistic locking (version) on DB records or serialize conflicting updates via locks. For ordering, generate unique id and persist; for inventory, use DB constraints and compensations.

27 — (T) (Tricky) If facade must both call sync payment and async inventory reservation, how to compose?

Answer: Option A: use saga with the facade initiating saga and returning pending response. Option B: perform inventory reserve synchronously first, then payment; if payment fails, release inventory (compensation). Design depends on business priorities.

28 — (T) (Tricky) How to handle multi-currency orders in facade pricing flows?

Answer: Facade accepts currency or client locale; call PricingService that uses currency conversion/regional taxes; pass currency to payment gateway and persist currency fields. Ensure rounding/presentation consistent.

29 — (T) (Tricky) How to implement SLO-aware behavior in facade (e.g., degrade if payment latency > threshold)?

Answer: Monitor downstream latency; if SLO breached, switch facade to degraded mode (queue orders, show maintenance message, or route to alternate provider). Use circuit-breaker metrics to trigger behavior.

30 — (T) (Tricky) How to design facade API to support idempotent retries from flaky clients?

Answer: Accept idempotencyKey and return prior result if seen. Persist mapping key → orderId/result. Enforce TTL for idempotency entries and cleanup policy.

31 — (T) (Tricky) How to make facade horizontally scalable while keeping unique order id allocation consistent?

Answer: Don’t use in-process counters; use DB sequence, Snowflake, or central ID service. Ensure facade instances are stateless to scale easily.

32 — (T) (Tricky) How to ensure facade doesn’t leak implementation details in error responses?

Answer: Map low-level exceptions to sanitized domain errors with clear codes and non-sensitive messages; log full details internally with correlation id.

33 — (T) (Tricky) How to gracefully handle schema changes in return payloads from facade?

Answer: Version APIs and deprecate fields over time. Provide backward-compatible transform layers or a translation facade for legacy clients.

34 — (T) (Tricky) How to test performance and load for the facade?

Answer: Run load tests simulating realistic call patterns (mocks for external services or test containers). Measure throughput, latency percentiles, resource usage, and backpressure handling.

35 — (T) (Tricky) How to instrument and trace across facade → saga → listeners for debugging?

Answer: Propagate correlation and trace ids through all calls and events. Use OpenTelemetry spans across facade entry, saga steps, and listeners; aggregate traces in tracing backend.

36 — (T) (Tricky) How to safely add a synchronous validation step (expensive) to facade that could slow down requests?

Answer: Move expensive validation to async pre-check (background job) or make it optional for fast path and run full validation asynchronously with eventual correction. Or cache validation results.

37 — (T) (Tricky) When facade must orchestrate transactions across two databases, what approach?

Answer: Avoid distributed transactions; implement saga compensation or transactional outbox + retryable consumers. If strong consistency required, centralize those operations into a single bounded service.

38 — (T) (Tricky) How to implement feature flags that change facade behavior per customer?

Answer: Inject FeatureToggleService into facade and branch behavior based on flags. Keep flag checks near routing/selection logic, not scattered throughout facade.

39 — (T) (Tricky) How to make facade resilient to cascading failures in dependency graph?

Answer: Use bulkhead isolation (thread pools per downstream), circuit-breakers, timeouts, and fallbacks. Prevent one slow dependency from consuming threads needed for others.

40 — (T) (Tricky) How to coordinate schema changes for data persisted by facade without downtime?

Answer: Use backward-compatible migrations, blue/green deployments, dual-write/dual-read during migration, and shepherded rollouts with feature flags.

41 — (T) (Tricky) How to handle request fragmentation if facade needs to call many downstream services with strict rate limits?

Answer: Batch, cache, and fan-out carefully. Use batching or aggregated queries where possible and apply per-downstream rate limiting to avoid hitting provider limits.

42 — (T) (Tricky) How to design facade to support multi-step checkout flows (save cart, confirm payment, finalize)?

Answer: Provide separate facade APIs: createOrderDraft(), confirmOrder(orderId, paymentInfo), finalizeShipment(). Each step persists state and can be resumed; the saga coordinates long-running workflows.

43 — (T) (Tricky) How to roll out a breaking change in facade while supporting older clients?

Answer: Introduce new API version; keep old version alive; add translation layer or adapter to map old payloads to new flows; provide migration guide and sunset timeline.

44 — (T) (Tricky) How to expose a facade for both internal services and third-party clients securely?

Answer: Use separate endpoints or API gateways with different authentication scopes and throttles. Internal clients may have higher quotas and extra access; third-party clients have stricter contracts.

45 — (T) (Tricky) How to design the facade to minimize the blast radius of security vulnerabilities?

Answer: Validate all inputs, enforce least privilege for credentials used by facade, externalize secrets with vaults, do input sanitization, and employ defense-in-depth (WAF, authN/Z).

46 — (T) (Tricky) How to handle eventual consistency expectations with clients using facade?

Answer: Document eventual guarantees: e.g., “order status becomes FINAL within X seconds/minutes” and provide status endpoints. Use explicit acknowledgement patterns (202 + callback) for asynchronous flows.

47 — (T) (Tricky) How to reduce coupling between facade and many downstream services when new ones appear frequently?

Answer: Use an internal service gateway or adapter layer; have facade depend on stable interfaces and let adapters evolve. Introduce a service registry for runtime resolution.

48 — (T) (Tricky) How to run tests that assert facade-level side effects (emails, inventory reservations) reliably?

Answer: Use test doubles or local emulators for external systems, or run integration tests with testcontainers and mocking stubs. Assert persisted state and messages in outbox or event broker.

49 — (T) (Tricky) How to design facade for high-availability during deployment / upgrade?

Answer: Use blue/green or canary deployments, ensure database migrations are backward-compatible, advertise rolling upgrades to clients, and make facade stateless so instances can be replaced without session stickiness.

50 — Summary — Facade best practices & pitfalls checklist

Answer:

Keep facade thin — orchestrate, don’t implement heavy business rules.

Document API semantics (sync/async, idempotency, error codes).

Use DI to inject dependencies and avoid static coupling.

Place transactional boundaries carefully; favor saga for cross-service consistency.

Implement idempotency keys and persistence for deduplication.

Use circuit-breakers, bulkheads and retries for resilience.

Instrument with metrics, structured logs, and distributed tracing.

Secure endpoints, rate-limit, and sanitize inputs.

Use versioning and feature flags for safe evolution.

Test unit, integration, and load; validate error and compensation paths.



50 In-Depth Interview Q&A — Flyweight (OrderService examples)

Context: In OrderService many OrderItem objects reference the same product metadata (name, description, image URL). A ProductMetaFactory returns shared ProductMeta flyweights to save memory and improve cache locality. Example:

public final class ProductMeta {
  public final String sku;
  public final String name;
  public final String description;
  private ProductMeta(String sku, String name, String description) { ... }
  // getters...
}

public class ProductMetaFactory {
  private final ConcurrentMap<String, ProductMeta> pool = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
  public ProductMeta get(String sku, Supplier<ProductMeta> loader){
    return pool.computeIfAbsent(sku, k -> loader.get());
  }
}

1 — What is the Flyweight pattern and why use it in OrderService?

Answer: Flyweight shares immutable, intrinsic state across many objects to reduce memory. In OrderService, ProductMeta (name/description/images) can be shared among thousands of OrderItem instances to drastically reduce memory footprint.

2 — What’s intrinsic vs extrinsic state in flyweight terms?

Answer: Intrinsic state is shareable and stored inside the flyweight (product name, sku). Extrinsic state varies per use and is passed in by clients (quantity, order-specific discount). Only intrinsic state is shared.

3 — Provide a thread-safe flyweight factory sketch (code).
class ProductMetaFactory {
  private final ConcurrentMap<String, ProductMeta> pool = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
  public ProductMeta get(String sku, Supplier<ProductMeta> loader){
    return pool.computeIfAbsent(sku, k -> loader.get());
  }
}


Use computeIfAbsent to avoid race conditions.

4 — How does Flyweight relate to memory and GC behavior?

Answer: It reduces object count, lowering GC pressure. But shared flyweights live as long as the pool references them, potentially increasing retention. Use weak references if you want GC reclaiming.

5 — When should you not use Flyweight?

Answer: Don’t use it for mutable state, for small object counts (no benefit), or if sharing complicates business logic. If extrinsic state is huge or costly to reconstruct, flyweight may harm clarity.

6 — How to test flyweight correctness?

Answer: Unit test that get(sku) returns the same instance for multiple calls and that extrinsic data passed to methods remains independent. Use identity (==) asserts.

7 — Should flyweights be immutable? Why?

Answer: Yes — immutability avoids shared-mutation bugs and makes caching safe. If flyweight needs occasional mutation, prefer copy-on-write or store mutable bits externally.

8 — How to handle large, rarely accessed flyweights (eviction)?

Answer: Use caches with eviction (Caffeine/Guava) or WeakReference/WeakHashMap so unused flyweights can be GCed. Tune cache size and TTL according to access patterns.

9 — Explain WeakReference vs strong reference in factory pool.

Answer: Strong references prevent GC (memory retained). WeakReference allows GC when no strong refs exist elsewhere — useful if flyweights are optional and memory is constrained. But weak refs can be reloaded causing churn.

10 — How to design flyweight keys? What should key uniqueness guarantee?

Answer: Keys must uniquely identify intrinsic state (e.g., SKU + version). If product data has multiple variants (color/size), include variant identifiers in key to avoid incorrect sharing.

11 — How does Flyweight affect serialization and persistence?

Answer: Serialize identity (SKU/version) rather than object graph. On deserialization, resolve to a flyweight via factory to maintain sharing semantics.

12 — How to handle flyweight updates (product metadata changes)?

Answer: Version flyweights: store a version number in the key (sku:v2). When metadata updates, create new flyweight and replace mapping. If using strong pool, ensure clients pick new versions (cache invalidation).

13 — What are the thread-safety concerns in a flyweight factory?

Answer: Race on creation (use computeIfAbsent), mutations to the pool (avoid non-thread-safe maps), and loader double-execution (ensure loader idempotence). Prefer concurrent collections.

14 — How to measure benefit of Flyweight?

Answer: Benchmark memory usage and GC before/after. Measure heap used, number of objects, and GC time under realistic load. Also measure latency impact of cache lookups.

15 — Is Flyweight useful in distributed systems (multiple nodes)? If so, how to maintain consistency?

Answer: On each node, a local flyweight cache reduces local memory. For consistency, versioning or centralized config (or event-driven invalidation) signals updates. Don’t attempt to share object identity across JVMs.

16 — How to combine Flyweight with Prototype / Builder?

Answer: Use Flyweight for intrinsic immutable parts; use Builder or Prototype to create per-order extrinsic state combined with the shared flyweight for final object creation.

17 — How to handle product images or large blobs in flyweights?

Answer: Store only metadata in flyweight and keep large blobs in a CDN. If you must store images, consider byte[] caching with limit/ eviction; or store references/URLs instead.

18 — How to avoid memory retention leaks with flyweight pools in long-running services?

Answer: Use size-bound caches, TTL eviction, or weakrefs. Monitor heap and retained set. Provide admin endpoints for cache purge on suspect stale data.

19 — How to ensure flyweights don’t leak sensitive data across tenants?

Answer: Include tenant id in flyweight key or maintain per-tenant factory instances. Never share tenant-scoped secrets in a global pool.

20 — How to support multi-version product metadata during migration?

Answer: Allow multiple flyweights keyed by version (sku:v1, sku:v2). Provide upgrade tools and background migration jobs to re-associate orders to new versions where required.

21 — How to load flyweights lazily or eagerly?

Answer: Lazy load when first requested (common). For hot products, pre-warm the cache at startup. Lazy keeps memory low but pays initial latency.

22 — How to handle the loader function failing during creation?

Answer: computeIfAbsent can throw. Ensure loader handles transient failures (retries) and exceptions map to domain errors. Avoid leaving inconsistent state in pool.

23 — How does Flyweight interact with equals/hashCode?

Answer: Flyweights implemented as immutable value objects should have robust equals/hashCode matching logical identity (sku+version). But identity checks (==) are used for shared-instance assumptions in tests.

24 — How to design API consumers so they pass extrinsic state correctly?

Answer: API should make it explicit: OrderItem { ProductMeta meta; int qty; BigDecimal priceOverride; } and document that meta is shared — clients must not mutate it.

25 — How to test concurrency around factory under heavy load?

Answer: Stress test multiple threads requesting same sku simultaneously and assert only one loader invocation (use counters) and no duplicate instances created.

26 — Flyweight vs cache: differences and overlap?

Answer: Flyweight is a structural pattern for sharing immutable intrinsic state; a cache is a more general mechanism. Practically, flyweight uses a cache under the hood — difference is conceptual.

27 — How to prevent double initialization if loader is slow?

Answer: Use computeIfAbsent or an explicit double-checked locking pattern with ConcurrentMap + Future/CompletableFuture as placeholder to avoid duplicate loads.

28 — How to handle fallback for missing product metadata?

Answer: Return a default ProductMeta (Null Object) with minimal info or throw ProductNotFoundException depending on business needs. Null Object avoids null checks downstream.

29 — How to instrument flyweight usage in production?

Answer: Record metrics: cache hits, misses, loader latency, pool size, eviction rate. Alert on high miss rates or loader latency spikes.

30 — How to make flyweight pool observability-friendly?

Answer: Expose endpoints with pool stats (size, hits/misses) and expose top skus consuming memory. Integrate with health checks and dashboards.

31 — How to deal with canonicalization of product attributes when creating keys?

Answer: Normalize keys (trim, lower-case) and include a canonical version of attributes to avoid duplicates due to inconsistent input strings.

32 — How to combine distributed caching (Redis) with local flyweight for memory and cluster-wide consistency?

Answer: Use Redis as authoritative metadata store; nodes use local flyweight cache for fast access and subscribe to pub/sub for invalidation on updates. Avoid storing large blobs in local cache if distribution is needed.

33 — Can Flyweight help with CPU/cache locality and performance, not just memory?

Answer: Yes — shared objects reduce cache churn and the cost of repeated object allocation. Fewer allocations means less GC and better CPU cache behavior.

34 — How to design the flyweight factory API for testability?

Answer: Make factory injectable and allow injection of a mock loader or a controllable map. Provide hooks to clear the pool in tests.

35 — How to secure flyweight metadata access (avoid data exfiltration)?

Answer: Control who can request certain skus (tenant isolation), avoid storing secrets in flyweights, and ensure logs do not leak sensitive fields from metadata.

36 — How to version flyweight behavior when mapping fields change (e.g., new tax rules)?

Answer: Keep mapping logic outside the flyweight (in services) and keep ProductMeta as data only. For data schema changes, version the flyweight payload and provide adapters for older consumers.

37 — How to garbage-collect flyweights only when safe?

Answer: Use WeakValueMap so flyweights are reclaimed when no outside strong refs exist. If flyweights must persist even with no outside refs, use strong references and manual eviction policies.

38 — How to handle concurrent cache invalidation across nodes?

Answer: Publish invalidation events (Kafka/Redis) when product metadata updates. Each node listens and evicts local flyweight entry. Use last-write-wins or version checks to avoid race conditions.

39 — How to handle flyweights for variant-rich products (size/color/region)?

Answer: Treat each variant as its own flyweight keyed by composite key (sku+variantId+region). Avoid over-sharing different variants under same flyweight.

40 — What are security/privacy pitfalls with flyweight caches?

Answer: Sharing metadata across tenants inadvertently if tenant id not included in key; persistent caches retaining stale PII; logging sensitive metadata. Ensure tenant isolation and redaction.

41 — How to perform warm-up/preload of flyweights at service startup?

Answer: Query top-N hot skus from DB or config and prepopulate pool asynchronously during startup to reduce first-request latency.

42 — How to design flyweight lifecycle (create, update, delete)?

Answer: Create on demand, update by creating a new versioned flyweight and evict old one, delete via eviction and optional tombstone to prevent reloading stale data immediately.

43 — How to implement transactional consistency if loader reads DB but DB changes mid-load?

Answer: Loader should read versioned data and check version after load. For critical consistency use DB snapshot or locks during load in rare cases; usually eventual consistency is acceptable.

44 — How to balance memory vs latency with eviction TTLs?

Answer: Short TTL reduces memory but increases loader calls (latency). Tune TTL based on access patterns: hot items long TTL; cold items short TTL. Consider second-level cache.

45 — How to handle metrics for flyweight storms (cache churn due to hot-spot updates)?

Answer: Detect high miss/eviction rates and alert. Use back-pressure on update rates, rate-limit metadata updates, and consider batching updates to reduce churn.

46 — How to design flyweight for analytics (counting how many items reference a flyweight)?

Answer: Maintain reference counters in the pool (AtomicInteger) incremented when clients create items and decremented on disposal; careful with leaks — prefer weak refs for automatic counting.

47 — (Tricky) How to avoid race between loader-insert and invalidation (update arrives during load)?

Answer: Use version-stamped keys: loader obtains latest version and sets mapping only if version matches. Or use placeholder Future in the map so invalidation can cancel/replace ongoing load.

48 — (Tricky) How to test flyweight eviction and reload behavior deterministically?

Answer: Use a testable cache impl (in-memory Caffeine) with controllable clock, or inject a mock scheduler to trigger eviction. Assert loader call counts and instance identity before/after eviction.

49 — (Tricky) How to maintain deterministic equality semantics across versions (old orders vs new metadata)?

Answer: Persist product metadata snapshot id alongside orders. Orders reference snapshot id to guarantee historical consistency, while live views use current flyweight for display if business allows.

50 — Best-practice checklist for Flyweight in OrderService

Use flyweight for large numbers of repeatable, immutable intrinsic objects (ProductMeta).

Keep flyweights immutable and small — extrinsic state lives in order item.

Use concurrent map + computeIfAbsent or a cache lib (Caffeine) for thread-safety and eviction.

Use versioned keys to handle metadata evolution and updates.

Choose strong vs weak references based on retention needs; prefer bounded caches with eviction in prod.

Pre-warm hot items, lazy-load others.

Avoid sharing across tenants; include tenant id if needed.

Instrument hits/misses, pool size, loader latency, and eviction rates.

Provide admin ops to clear/refresh cache, and pub/sub invalidation for cluster consistency.

Test concurrency, eviction, and update scenarios; measure memory and GC impact.


50 In-Depth Interview Q&A — Proxy (OrderService examples)

Context: A Proxy implements the same interface as a real subject but adds extra behavior (caching, lazy init, access control, remote call marshalling). Example: a caching OrderRepositoryProxy that wraps a JpaOrderRepository.

public interface OrderRepository { Order findById(String id); Order save(Order o); }

public class OrderRepositoryProxy implements OrderRepository {
  private final OrderRepository delegate;
  private final Cache<String, Order> cache;
  public OrderRepositoryProxy(OrderRepository delegate, Cache cache) {...}
  public Order findById(String id){
    return cache.get(id, () -> delegate.findById(id));
  }
  public Order save(Order o){
    Order saved = delegate.save(o);
    cache.put(saved.getId(), saved);
    return saved;
  }
}

1 — (S) What is the Proxy pattern and why use it in OrderService?

Answer: Proxy provides a surrogate for another object implementing the same interface, adding behavior (caching, access control, lazy init, remote marshalling) without changing the real object. Use it to add non-functional features around OrderRepository, PaymentGateway, or remote services.

2 — (S) Give three common proxy types and a short OrderService example for each.

Answer:

Caching (Virtual) Proxy: caches Order reads to reduce DB hits.

Protection Proxy: checks user permissions before allowing cancelOrder().

Remote Proxy: stubs local calls to remote Order microservice (handles serialization).

3 — (S) Proxy vs Decorator — what's the difference?

Answer: Both wrap objects with same interface. Intent differs: Proxy controls access to or representation of the subject (lazy init, remote access), while Decorator adds behavior/feature augmentation. In practice they overlap.

4 — (I) How to implement an access-control (protection) proxy for order operations?

Answer: Proxy checks caller identity/roles and delegates only if allowed; otherwise throws AccessDeniedException. Keep authorization policy outside proxy or inject policy service to avoid hardcoding.

public Order get(String id, User user){
  if(!authz.canView(user, id)) throw new AccessDenied();
  return delegate.get(id);
}

5 — (I) How to build a caching proxy safely for writes? (write-through vs write-behind)

Answer: Write-through: proxy writes to DB then updates cache (simpler, consistent). Write-behind: write to cache and flush async — higher performance but risk of loss. For orders prefer write-through or outbox pattern to ensure durability.

6 — (I) How to handle read-after-write consistency with cache proxy?

Answer: Immediately update cache after save() (write-through). For distributed caches, ensure cache and DB are updated atomically (transactional outbox or using DB as source-of-truth and invalidate cache on commit).

7 — (I) How to implement a lazy-initializing (virtual) proxy for heavy InvoiceService?

Answer: Proxy holds a supplier to construct InvoiceService on first call. Useful for expensive remote clients or when service rarely used.

public class LazyInvoiceProxy implements InvoiceService {
  private volatile InvoiceService delegate;
  private synchronized void init(){ if(delegate==null) delegate = new RealInvoiceService(); }
  public Invoice generate(Order o){ init(); return delegate.generate(o); }
}

8 — (I) How to test a proxy in isolation?

Answer: Mock the delegate and any cross-cutting services (cache, auth). Verify proxy behavior: cache hits/misses, permission checks, lazy init calls, error mapping. Use verify() to assert delegate invocation counts.

9 — (I) How to implement a remote proxy and what are key concerns?

Answer: Remote proxy marshals local method calls to RPC/HTTP, handles serialization, retries, timeouts, and error translation. Concerns: network failure, latency, idempotency, security (auth/tokens), and versioning.

10 — (I) How to add metrics/tracing to proxy-wrapped calls?

Answer: Proxy starts a trace/span and records latency, success/failure, and other tags (orderId, method). Use Micrometer/OpenTelemetry in proxy to get per-endpoint metrics.

11 — (M) (Senior) How to design a proxy for multi-tenant rate limiting?

Answer: Proxy enforces per-tenant rate limits using a distributed token bucket (Redis). On limit exceed, return informative 429. Keep counters sharded and use local leaky-buckets for short-term smoothing.

12 — (M) How to combine proxy with circuit-breaker/resilience patterns?

Answer: Proxy delegates calls to a resilience wrapper (Resilience4j) which performs circuit-breaker, retries, fallback. Compose: new CircuitBreakerProxy(new RemoteProxy(...)) or integrate resiliency inside proxy.

13 — (M) How to handle cache invalidation race between proxy and concurrent updates?

Answer: Use DB-level transactions or versioning with compare-and-swap for cache updates; perform invalidation after successful commit; use outbox pattern to reliably propagate invalidation events.

14 — (M) How to avoid proxy creating bottlenecks (single-threaded init, heavy locking)?

Answer: Use lock-free initialization patterns (volatile + double-checked locking or holder idiom), and avoid coarse-grained synchronized blocks. For expensive operations, use CompletableFuture placeholders.

15 — (M) How to make proxies composable and configurable in Spring?

Answer: Build proxies as beans composed in @Configuration. Use factory that applies decorators/proxies conditionally. Use proxy factories or ProxyFactoryBean for AOP-style proxies.

16 — (M) How to avoid the Service Locator anti-pattern when wiring proxies?

Answer: Inject the delegate and cross-cutting dependencies into the proxy via DI. Avoid proxies pulling dependencies from global contexts.

17 — (M) How to implement a smart-reference proxy that manages resource life-cycle?

Answer: Proxy performs connection pooling, lazy open/close, and eviction. For example, DB client proxy that opens connection on first call and reuses pool, closing on shutdown.

18 — (M) How to version remote interfaces with proxies for backward compatibility?

Answer: Proxy maps local calls to the appropriate remote API version (adapter logic). Support multiple remote versions by selecting correct marshaller based on target endpoint or feature flags.

19 — (M) How to implement authorization caching in a protection proxy without stale permissions?

Answer: Cache permission checks with short TTLs or use cache invalidation on role changes. For critical ops, skip cache and query authZ service live.

20 — (M) How to implement a fallback proxy that routes to an alternate provider?

Answer: On failure, proxy can call a fallback delegate (another provider) with same idempotency semantics. Persist outcome to avoid double processing and log fallback use.

21 — (T) (Tricky) How to guarantee no double-charge when a proxy retries a remote payment call?

Answer: Use idempotency keys passed through proxy to gateway; gateway must honor deduplication. Proxy should persist request + idempotency mapping so retries reuse same key and result.

22 — (T) (Tricky) How to handle serialization compatibility when proxy talks to multiple remote versions?

Answer: Implement version-aware serializers and DTO mappers. Proxy detects remote version (endpoint or handshake) and selects appropriate (de)serializer; maintain adapters for each version.

23 — (T) (Tricky) Remote proxy vs local proxy in microservices — which to prefer and why?

Answer: Use remote proxy for transparent RPC (convenient but hides network failures); prefer explicit client services for clarity. Remote proxy is OK for thin client stubs but ensure robustness (timeouts, retries, fallbacks).

24 — (T) (Tricky) How to implement caching proxy that supports conditional GET and ETags?

Answer: Proxy stores ETag with cached resource. On subsequent get, send If-None-Match header to remote. If 304 returned, use cached version. This reduces payloads while keeping freshness.

25 — (T) (Tricky) How to ensure thread-safety if proxy caches mutable objects?

Answer: Return defensive copies from cache or store immutable snapshots. Do not expose mutable cached objects directly—otherwise different callers mutate shared state.

26 — (T) (Tricky) How to handle failover when the delegate node is partitioned?

Answer: Proxy can consult service discovery to pick another healthy delegate instance. Use circuit-breaker and fallback to secondary region/provider. Persist requests for later reconciliation if needed.

27 — (T) (Tricky) How to implement access control proxy that supports attribute-based access control (ABAC)?

Answer: Proxy calls policy engine (OPA) with request context, resource attributes, and returns permit/deny. Cache common decisions and handle policy changes via short TTLs.

28 — (T) (Tricky) How to avoid long tail latencies introduced by proxies?

Answer: Keep proxy code minimal and non-blocking. Use async IO, bounded thread pools, and timeouts; don't perform heavy work synchronously in request path.

29 — (T) (Tricky) How to instrument a proxy to diagnose production issues?

Answer: Log per-request correlation id, delegate target, latency, errors, and cache hits. Emit structured metrics for hit/miss, retries, fallback counts, and circuit-breaker state.

30 — (T) (Tricky) How to proxy streaming responses (large order history) efficiently?

Answer: Use streaming (Reactive or chunked HTTP) so proxy forwards chunks instead of buffering full response. Map backpressure from downstream to upstream when possible.

31 — (M) How to combine proxy with transactional outbox for safe event publishing?

Answer: Proxy ensures DB write then writes event to outbox within same transaction. Outbox dispatcher publishes events. Proxy itself calls only DB + outbox write, avoiding direct broker calls in request path.

32 — (M) How to implement a proxy that provides request hedging (send to multiple delegates and take fastest)?

Answer: Proxy sends the same request to primary and secondary concurrently (with limits), returns first successful reply, and cancels others. Use carefully to avoid double side effects—only safe for idempotent reads or when deduplication is supported.

33 — (M) How to handle timeouts and cancellation in proxy for long-running operations?

Answer: Proxy enforces client deadline; propagate cancellation to delegate via interruption or cancellation token; release resources promptly and return meaningful timeout error.

34 — (M) How to test behavior of proxies under network partitions and latency?

Answer: Use fault injection tools (Chaos Monkey, Toxiproxy) in integration tests to simulate partitions, latency, and errors; assert proxy fallbacks and resilience.

35 — (M) How to manage per-tenant proxy configuration (different caches, rate limits)?

Answer: Use tenant-aware proxy instances or inject tenant config into proxy and use per-tenant keys in caches and rate-limiters. Keep tenant state isolated to avoid leakage.

36 — (M) How to avoid proxy code bloating with cross-cutting concerns?

Answer: Compose proxies with small single-purpose proxies (caching, auth, metrics) rather than monolithic proxies. Use chain-of-responsibility or proxy factory to assemble the chain.

37 — (M) How to replace a proxy at runtime without restart?

Answer: Use an indirection (volatile reference) to current proxy instance. Swap atomically to new proxy implementation; ensure old instance remains valid for in-flight requests.

38 — (M) How to ensure the proxy does not leak credentials or sensitive data in logs?

Answer: Redact sensitive fields before logging; avoid dumping full request bodies. Use dedicated audit logs with limited access if full payloads must be stored.

39 — (M) How to implement a metrics-backed adaptive proxy (adjust behavior based on observed errors)?

Answer: Proxy reads metrics (error rate, latency) and dynamically adjusts behavior (lower concurrency, more aggressive caching, route to fallback). Use control loops and safety thresholds.

40 — (T) (Tricky) Proxy that buffers requests during brief DB outage — how to avoid unbounded memory growth?

Answer: Use bounded queue with backpressure; reject when full with clear error to client. Persist buffered requests to disk/queue (durable buffer) if you must survive restarts.

41 — (T) (Tricky) How to implement a transparent proxy for API version negotiation?

Answer: Proxy inspects client API version, transforms request to backend expected version and transforms response back. Maintain mapping logic modularly and use contract tests to validate mappings.

42 — (T) (Tricky) When proxying across network boundaries, how to manage TLS and cert rotation?

Answer: Proxy should use TLS, validate server certs, and support dynamic rotation of client certs via secret manager. Hot-reload certs without restart and handle handshake failures gracefully.

43 — (T) (Tricky) How to implement debug-mode proxy that records full request/response for a specific request id?

Answer: Proxy checks request id against debug list and captures full payloads to secure storage; ensure sampling and TTL for stored data and strict access control for debug logs.

44 — (T) (Tricky) Proxy that must guarantee ordering for related requests — how to do it?

Answer: Partition requests by ordering key and send each partition through a single-threaded executor per key; this preserves ordering at cost of potential head-of-line blocking.

45 — (T) (Tricky) How to avoid inconsistent behavior when proxy does asynchronous cache refresh?

Answer: Use stale-while-revalidate pattern: serve stale content with background revalidation while ensuring revalidation is idempotent and throttle revalidation frequency to avoid stampede.

46 — (T) (Tricky) Proxy with hybrid local+distributed cache — how to maintain coherence?

Answer: Use local cache for speed and distributed cache for coherence; use pub/sub to broadcast invalidation events so local caches evict stale entries. Consider consistent hashing to reduce invalidations.

47 — (T) (Tricky) Proxy that applies A/B routing per request — how to ensure consistency across retries?

Answer: Assign persistent routing decision (sticky) based on user id or idempotency key so retries go to the same variant. Store routing decision in a small lookup table with TTL.

48 — (T) (Tricky) How to debug subtle proxy ordering bugs in production?

Answer: Correlate traces and logs by request id across proxy and delegate. Add diagnostic logging for queue and thread pool states. Reproduce with load-tests and slightly different timings to expose race conditions.

49 — (T) (Tricky) How to secure a proxy management endpoint (clear cache, swap proxies)?

Answer: Protect with strong authZ, require multi-step confirmation for destructive ops, log and audit admin actions, and allow role-based access. Provide dry-run and preview for changes.

50 — Summary — best-practices & pitfalls checklist for Proxy in OrderService

Answer:

Choose correct proxy type for the use-case (caching, protection, remote, virtual).

Keep proxies single-purpose and composable; assemble chains via factories.

Ensure thread-safety (concurrent maps, atomic swaps) and avoid blocking init.

For caching proxies: prefer write-through or outbox for consistency; use bounded caches and evictions.

For remote proxies: implement timeouts, retries, idempotency keys, and error mapping.

Instrument proxies (metrics/traces) and expose health checks.

Secure proxies (authZ, certs, redaction).

Test proxies with unit, contract, and chaos tests (fault injection).

Provide admin controls for cache eviction and safe runtime swaps.

Monitor and tune to avoid proxies becoming bottlenecks or single points of failure.



50 In-Depth Interview Q&A — Chain of Responsibility (OrderService examples)

Context: CoR passes a request along a chain of handlers until one handles it (or all do). In OrderService we use a DiscountChain where multiple DiscountHandlers examine an Order and apply discounts, possibly stopping the chain if an exclusive discount is applied.

Example interface:

interface DiscountHandler {
  boolean apply(Order order); // returns true if handled and chain should stop
}
class DiscountChain {
  private final List<DiscountHandler> handlers;
  public void handle(Order order){
    for(DiscountHandler h : handlers) {
      boolean stop = h.apply(order);
      if(stop) break;
    }
  }
}

1 — What is Chain of Responsibility and why use it in OrderService?

Answer: CoR decouples senders and receivers by letting multiple handlers try to process a request. Use it to implement extensible processing pipelines (discounts, validations, enrichment) where rules may be added/removed/configured without changing the pipeline consumer.

2 — Simple discount chain example: how does it work on an Order?

Answer: Each DiscountHandler checks order conditions (promo code, loyalty tier) and applies adjustments to order.total. Some handlers return true to stop further discounts (exclusive promotions), others return false to let the chain continue.

3 — How does CoR support SOLID principles?

Answer: SRP: handlers focus on one rule. OCP: add new handlers without modifying chain client. DIP: higher-level code depends on DiscountHandler interface. ISP: handlers expose minimal contracts.

4 — How to wire a discount chain with Spring so handlers are discovered automatically?

Answer: Implement handlers as @Component and inject List<DiscountHandler> in order (use @Order or Ordered); compose chain by iterating list. This keeps registration declarative.

5 — When should a handler stop the chain versus allow other handlers to run?

Answer: Stop when a rule is exclusive (e.g., “apply 100% off VIP coupon — no other discounts”), or when handling indicates finality. Otherwise allow continuation to combine discounts or apply accumulative rules.

6 — Show how to pass context and results along the chain without global state.

Answer: Use a ProcessingContext object passed through:

class DiscountContext { Order order; Map<String,Object> metadata; }
boolean apply(DiscountContext ctx);


Handlers add metadata not global variables.

7 — How to unit-test individual handlers?

Answer: Inject a test Order into the handler and assert the expected changes (total reduction, flags). Test both positive and negative conditions, and test that handler returns correct stop flag when required.

8 — How to test the full chain behavior?

Answer: Compose real handlers in a test chain and assert final order.total and the sequence of handlers invoked (use spies/mocks or instrument handlers to record invocation order).

9 — How to handle exceptions thrown by handlers?

Answer: Decide policy: either let exceptions bubble to caller (fail-fast) or catch in chain, record error in context, and either stop or continue. For transient errors, consider marking chain processing as retryable.
