        public String getName() { return name; }

        @Override
        public void accept(OrderVisitor visitor) {
            visitor.visit(this); // visitor handles bundle (and usually recurses)
        }

        @Override
        public String toString() {
            return "Bundle[" + name + ", count=" + children.size() + "]";
        }
    }

    // Root structure (convenience)
    public static class Order implements OrderComponent {
        private final List<OrderComponent> components = new ArrayList<>();
        public void add(OrderComponent c) { components.add(c); }
        public List<OrderComponent> getComponents() { return components; }

        @Override
        public void accept(OrderVisitor visitor) {
            // Elements-driven traversal: order delegates traversal to elements
            visitor.beforeOrder(this);
            for (OrderComponent c : components) {
                c.accept(visitor);
            }
            visitor.afterOrder(this);
        }
    }

    // ---------- Visitor interface ----------
    public interface OrderVisitor {
        // visit methods for each concrete element type:
        void visit(OrderItem item);
        void visit(Bundle bundle);

        // optional lifecycle hooks:
        default void beforeOrder(Order order) {}
        default void afterOrder(Order order) {}
    }

    // ---------- Concrete Visitor: PricingVisitor ----------
    public static class PricingVisitor implements OrderVisitor {
        private BigDecimal total = BigDecimal.ZERO;
        private final List<String> lines = new ArrayList<>();
        // simple indentation to reflect nesting
        private int depth = 0;

        public BigDecimal getTotal() { return total; }
        public List<String> getLines() { return lines; }

        @Override
        public void beforeOrder(Order order) {
            lines.add("Start pricing order");
        }

        @Override
        public void afterOrder(Order order) {
            lines.add("End pricing order");
        }

        @Override
        public void visit(OrderItem item) {
            BigDecimal line = item.getUnitPrice().multiply(new BigDecimal(item.getQty()));
            total = total.add(line);
            lines.add(indent() + String.format("- %s x%d @ %s = %s",
                    item.getSku(), item.getQty(), item.getUnitPrice(), line));
        }

        @Override
        public void visit(Bundle bundle) {
            lines.add(indent() + "+ Bundle: " + bundle.getName());
            depth++;
            // explicit recursion: elements-driven could have bundle recurse, but we do it here
            for (OrderComponent c : bundle.getChildren()) {
                c.accept(this); // recurse into children
            }
            depth--;
            lines.add(indent() + "-- end bundle: " + bundle.getName());
        }

        private String indent() {
            return "  ".repeat(Math.max(0, depth));
        }
    }
}

Short explanation & interview-friendly bullets

What happens (double-dispatch): order.accept(pricingVisitor) → Order iterates components → each OrderItem/Bundle calls visitor.visit(this). The visitor implementation executes logic based on the concrete element type — that's double dispatch (visitor type + element type).

Why use Visitor here: you can add new operations (pricing, tax, invoice generation, analytics) by writing new visitors, without changing the OrderItem/Bundle classes.

When not to use: if you frequently add new element types (adding a new element requires updating every visitor), or when per-node side effects need transactional guarantees (visitors often are best used to produce DTOs/change-sets which are then applied by services).

Extensibility tip: inject strategies/services into visitors (e.g., TaxService into TaxVisitor) rather than letting visitors call global state; this keeps visitors testable and portable.


Thanks — I pulled content from your uploaded notes and wrote **plain-text, interview-ready entries** for topics **13 → 21** (HTTPS & Browser Behavior through Architecture & Best Practices). I’ve kept each item concise, actionable, and with one short example or config where helpful. I also included file citations to the parts of your uploaded document I used. Paste/append these into your master file or use them as separate handouts.

---

13. HTTPS & Browser Behavior

* **What happens (high level):** Browser resolves DNS → opens TCP connection → for HTTPS performs TLS handshake (certificate validation, key exchange) → sends encrypted HTTP request → server responds over the same TLS session. Browser then renders HTML/CSS/JS, executes scripts, applies CSP/cookies/SameSite, and may cache static assets.&#x20;
* **Why it matters for security:** HTTPS prevents eavesdropping, tampering, and MITM attacks (including SSL-stripping if HSTS not used). TLS certificate validity and correct cipher configuration are essential.&#x20;
* **Common browser-level attack points:** DNS spoofing, SSL stripping (downgrade), cookie theft via XSS, cache poisoning, leaking sensitive query params in referers or logs.&#x20;
* **Practical defenses:** Enforce HTTPS site-wide; set HSTS (Strict-Transport-Security); use secure, HttpOnly, SameSite cookies for session tokens; avoid putting secrets in URLs; configure CSP to reduce XSS risk; limit caching for sensitive responses (Cache-Control: no-store).

---

14. Request Mapping (Spring MVC)

* **What it is:** `@RequestMapping`, `@GetMapping`, `@PostMapping`, etc., bind HTTP requests to controller methods. Query params (`@RequestParam`), path vars (`@PathVariable`), and request bodies (`@RequestBody`) determine how input reaches the app.&#x20;
* **Where attacks appear:** Any mapped parameter that ends up used in SQL, filesystem paths, or dynamic HTML can be abused (e.g., SQLi via query params, path traversal via poorly validated path vars, XSS via returned content).
* **Secure patterns:** Always validate/bind inputs (`@Valid`, DTOs), prefer `@RequestBody` with DTO validation for complex payloads, avoid directly using request params in SQL or as identifiers without whitelist validation. For dynamic identifiers (ORDER BY, column names), use strict whitelists.
* **Example:** Don’t do `String q = "SELECT ... WHERE id=" + idParam;` — use prepared statements or JPA parameter binding instead.&#x20;

---

15. Session Management & Tokens

* **Options:** Server sessions (session id in cookie) vs stateless tokens (JWT) vs hybrid (rotate refresh tokens). Cookies should be `Secure`, `HttpOnly`, and `SameSite`.
* **Threats:** Session fixation, session hijacking (cookie theft via XSS), token theft in localStorage (XSS exposure), replay attacks.
* **Best practices:** Regenerate session IDs on privilege change (login), short session TTLs, use refresh tokens with rotation, store tokens in secure HttpOnly cookies (avoid localStorage for sensitive tokens), implement logout and revocation logic, implement IP/device anomaly detection and MFA for sensitive actions.

---

16. Error Handling & Logging

* **Principles:** Never expose detailed internal errors to users. Log detailed stack traces and DB errors to internal logs/secure SIEM, but return generic user-facing messages. This prevents info leakage that can help an attacker craft SQLi/XSS payloads.&#x20;
* **Logging hygiene:** Avoid logging secrets (passwords, tokens). Sanitize logged user input to avoid log injection. Correlate logs with request IDs/traces for incident analysis. Forward logs to centralized logging (ELK/EFK, Splunk) for detection rules and SIEM alerts.
* **Error-handling patterns:** Use global exception handlers (`@ControllerAdvice` + `@ExceptionHandler`), produce consistent error payloads and status codes, and separate operational vs application errors in logs.&#x20;

---

17. Security Tools (WAF, etc.)

* **WAF (Web Application Firewall):** Blocks common attack patterns (SQLi, known XSS payloads, known scanners). Good as a layer, but not a substitute for secure code. Tune to reduce false positives/negatives.
* **Other tools:** RASP (Runtime Application Self-Protection) for in-app anomaly detection; API gateways for rate-limiting and auth; IDS/IPS for network anomalies; secret managers (Vault) for credentials.&#x20;
* **Practical setup:** Put WAF at edge, API gateway for token validation and throttling, and RASP where possible to detect in-process attacks. Regularly update WAF signatures and tune rules to your app’s traffic patterns.&#x20;

---

18. Attack Vectors (XSS, CSRF)

* **XSS (Cross-Site Scripting):** Occurs when untrusted input is included in pages without proper escaping, letting attackers run scripts in victim browsers. Prevent with proper output encoding/escaping, input validation, and strong CSP headers. Prefer frameworks that auto-escape templates.
* **CSRF (Cross-Site Request Forgery):** Attacker tricks an authenticated user into making unwanted state-changing requests. Prevent with anti-CSRF tokens, use of SameSite=strict/lax cookies, and requiring origin/referrer checks for sensitive endpoints. For APIs, prefer stateless tokens in Authorization headers (not cookie-auth) or implement double-submit cookies.&#x20;
* **Quick mitigations:** CSP, escaping, input validation for XSS; CSRF tokens + SameSite cookies + re-auth for critical actions for CSRF.

---

19. Input Validation & Sanitization

* **Principle:** Validate *both* client- and server-side. Server-side validation is authoritative. Use whitelisting (allowed patterns) over blacklisting. Use annotations (`@Valid`, `@NotNull`, `@Size`, `@Email`) and strict regexes where applicable.
* **Sanitization:** Escape outputs for the appropriate context (HTML, JS, SQL). For HTML use template escaping; for SQL use parameter binding. For file paths, normalize and whitelist allowed directories to prevent traversal. For `LIKE` queries, escape `%`/`_` characters before binding.
* **Testing:** Add SAST rules (SonarQube), automated unit tests for validation logic, and dynamic tests (OWASP ZAP/Burp) in CI.&#x20;

---

20. Database Security (Prepared Statements, etc.)

* **Primary defense:** Parameterized queries / PreparedStatements — always. Avoid string concatenation for SQL. Use ORM parameter binding (JPQL `:param`, Criteria API) or stored procedures with bound parameters.
* **Additional hardening:** Disable multi-statement execution in drivers (e.g., `allowMultiQueries=false`), use least-privilege DB users (read-only vs write/admin roles), encrypt DB connections (TLS), and keep DB software patched. Log DB activity and monitor for anomalous queries (DBAM).
* **Edge cases:** Safely handle dynamic `IN(...)` lists by building parameter placeholders and binding each item; never bind identifiers—use whitelists for column/table names. Escaping `LIKE` patterns and using array binds where supported (Postgres) is recommended.

---

21. Architecture & Best Practices

* **Defense-in-depth:** Combine secure coding (prepared statements, escaping), infrastructure controls (WAF, TLS, HSTS), and monitoring (central logs, SIEM, DB activity monitoring). No single control is sufficient.
* **Design practices:** Use layered services: API gateway for auth/rate-limit, service mesh/mTLS for service-to-service auth, and RBAC/least-privilege for DB/service accounts. Externalize config & secrets (Vault), use CI/CD with security gates (SAST/DAST), and automated tests (unit + integration + security scans).
* **Operational practices:** Centralized logging & tracing, alerting on anomalies (slow responses indicative of time-based blind-SQLi), regular pen tests, and blue/green or canary deploys for safe rollouts. Emphasize secure defaults (deny-by-default), documented threat models, and regular dependency patching.
